<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Optics</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">This article is about the branch of physics. For the book by Sir Isaac Newton, see <a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Optic_(disambiguation)" title="Optic (disambiguation)">Optic (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2818" data-file-width="2309" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/220px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/330px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/440px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Optics includes study of <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">dispersion</a> of light.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Optics</b> is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> which involves the behaviour and properties of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>, including its interactions with <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and the construction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_instruments" title="Optical instruments">instruments</a> that use or <a href="/wiki/Photodetector" title="Photodetector">detect</a> it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGrawHill_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGrawHill-1">[1]</a></sup> Optics usually describes the behaviour of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> light. Because light is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">electromagnetic wave</a>, other forms of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> such as <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwaves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a> exhibit similar properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGrawHill_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-McGrawHill-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most optical phenomena can be accounted for using the <a href="/wiki/Classical_electromagnetism" title="Classical electromagnetism">classical electromagnetic</a> description of light. Complete electromagnetic descriptions of light are, however, often difficult to apply in practice. Practical optics is usually done using simplified models. The most common of these, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geometric_optics" title="Geometric optics">geometric optics</a>, treats light as a collection of <a href="/wiki/Ray_(optics)" title="Ray (optics)">rays</a> that travel in straight lines and bend when they pass through or reflect from surfaces. <a href="/wiki/Physical_optics" title="Physical optics">Physical optics</a> is a more comprehensive model of light, which includes <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">wave</a> effects such as <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(optics)" title="Interference (optics)">interference</a> that cannot be accounted for in geometric optics. Historically, the ray-based model of light was developed first, followed by the wave model of light. Progress in electromagnetic theory in the 19th century led to the discovery that light waves were in fact electromagnetic radiation.</p>
<p>Some phenomena depend on the fact that light has both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave-particle_duality" title="Wave-particle duality">wave-like and particle-like properties</a>. Explanation of these effects requires <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. When considering light's particle-like properties, the light is modelled as a collection of particles called "<a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>". <a href="/wiki/Quantum_optics" title="Quantum optics">Quantum optics</a> deals with the application of quantum mechanics to optical systems.</p>
<p>Optical science is relevant to and studied in many related disciplines including <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> fields, <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmology" title="Ophthalmology">ophthalmology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optometry" title="Optometry">optometry</a>). Practical applications of optics are found in a variety of technologies and everyday objects, including <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">mirrors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lenses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">telescopes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscopes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibre_optics" title="Fibre optics">fibre optics</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_optics" title="History of optics">History of optics</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_electromagnetism_and_classical_optics" title="Timeline of electromagnetism and classical optics">Timeline of electromagnetism and classical optics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nimrud_lens_British_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="926" data-file-width="1040" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Nimrud_lens_British_Museum.jpg/220px-Nimrud_lens_British_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Nimrud_lens_British_Museum.jpg/330px-Nimrud_lens_British_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Nimrud_lens_British_Museum.jpg/440px-Nimrud_lens_British_Museum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nimrud_lens_British_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Nimrud lens</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Optics began with the development of lenses by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamians</a>. The earliest known lenses, made from polished crystal, often <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, date from as early as 700 BC for <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> lenses such as the Layard/<a href="/wiki/Nimrud_lens" title="Nimrud lens">Nimrud lens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman" title="Ancient Roman">ancient Romans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> filled glass spheres with water to make lenses. These practical developments were followed by the development of theories of light and vision by ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian</a> philosophers, and the development of <a href="/wiki/Geometrical_optics" title="Geometrical optics">geometrical optics</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a>. The word <i>optics</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">ancient Greek</a> word <span lang="grc">ὀπτική</span> (<i>optikē</i>), meaning "appearance, look".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a> on optics broke down into two opposing theories on how vision worked, the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intromission_theory" title="Intromission theory">intromission theory</a>" and the <a href="/wiki/Emission_theory_(vision)" title="Emission theory (vision)">"emission theory"</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The intro-mission approach saw vision as coming from objects casting off copies of themselves (called eidola) that were captured by the eye. With many propagators including <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and their followers, this theory seems to have some contact with modern theories of what vision really is, but it remained only speculation lacking any experimental foundation.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> first articulated the emission theory, the idea that <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">visual perception</a> is accomplished by rays emitted by the eyes. He also commented on the <a href="/wiki/Parity_(physics)" title="Parity (physics)">parity</a> reversal of mirrors in <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Some hundred years later, <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> wrote a treatise entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid#Other_works" title="Euclid">Optics</a></i> where he linked vision to <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a>, creating <i>geometrical optics</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> He based his work on Plato's emission theory wherein he described the mathematical rules of <a href="/wiki/Perspective_(graphical)" title="Perspective (graphical)">perspective</a> and described the effects of <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a> qualitatively, although he questioned that a beam of light from the eye could instantaneously light up the stars every time someone blinked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, in his treatise <i><a href="/wiki/Ptolemy#Optics" title="Ptolemy">Optics</a></i>, held an extramission-intromission theory of vision: the rays (or flux) from the eye formed a cone, the vertex being within the eye, and the base defining the visual field. The rays were sensitive, and conveyed information back to the observer’s intellect about the distance and orientation of surfaces. He summarised much of Euclid and went on to describe a way to measure the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angle_of_refraction" title="Angle of refraction">angle of refraction</a>, though he failed to notice the empirical relationship between it and the angle of incidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ptolemy_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ptolemy-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alhazen,_the_Persian.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="302" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Alhazen%2C_the_Persian.gif/170px-Alhazen%2C_the_Persian.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Alhazen%2C_the_Persian.gif/255px-Alhazen%2C_the_Persian.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Alhazen%2C_the_Persian.gif 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alhazen,_the_Persian.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a> (Ibn al-Haytham), "the father of Optics"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ibn_Sahl_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1471" data-file-width="1139" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Ibn_Sahl_manuscript.jpg/170px-Ibn_Sahl_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Ibn_Sahl_manuscript.jpg/255px-Ibn_Sahl_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Ibn_Sahl_manuscript.jpg/340px-Ibn_Sahl_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ibn_Sahl_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reproduction of a page of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Sahl" title="Ibn Sahl">Ibn Sahl</a>'s manuscript showing his knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell's law">the law of refraction</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, Greek ideas about optics were resurrected and extended by writers in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>. One of the earliest of these was <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a> (c. 801–73) who wrote on the merits of Aristotelian and Euclidean ideas of optics, favouring the emission theory since it could better quantify optical phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In 984, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persian</a> mathematician <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Sahl" title="Ibn Sahl">Ibn Sahl</a> wrote the treatise "On burning mirrors and lenses", correctly describing a law of refraction equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell's law">Snell's law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-j1-11">[11]</a></sup> He used this law to compute optimum shapes for lenses and <a href="/wiki/Curved_mirror" title="Curved mirror">curved mirrors</a>. In the early 11th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a> (Ibn al-Haytham) wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Optics" title="Book of Optics">Book of Optics</a></i> (<i>Kitab al-manazir</i>) in which he explored reflection and refraction and proposed a new system for explaining vision and light based on observation and experiment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> He rejected the "emission theory" of Ptolemaic optics with its rays being emitted by the eye, and instead put forward the idea that light reflected in all directions in straight lines from all points of the objects being viewed and then entered the eye, although he was unable to correctly explain how the eye captured the rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Alhazen's work was largely ignored in the Arabic world but it was anonymously translated into Latin around 1200 A.D. and further summarised and expanded on by the Polish monk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Witelo" title="Witelo">Witelo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> making it a standard text on optics in Europe for the next 400 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 13th century in medieval Europe, English bishop <a href="/wiki/Robert_Grosseteste" title="Robert Grosseteste">Robert Grosseteste</a> wrote on a wide range of scientific topics, and discussed light from four different perspectives: an <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> of light, a <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">cosmogony</a> of light, an <a href="/wiki/Etiology" title="Etiology">etiology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> of light, and a <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> of light,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> basing it on the works Aristotle and Platonism. Grosseteste's most famous disciple, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a>, wrote works citing a wide range of recently translated optical and philosophical works, including those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a>, Aristotle, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>, Euclid, al-Kindi, Ptolemy, Tideus, and <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_African" title="Constantine the African">Constantine the African</a>. Bacon was able to use parts of glass spheres as <a href="/wiki/Magnifying_glass" title="Magnifying glass">magnifying glasses</a> to demonstrate that light reflects from objects rather than being released from them.</p>
<p>The first wearable eyeglasses were invented in Italy around 1286.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This was the start of the optical industry of grinding and polishing lenses for these "spectacles", first in Venice and Florence in the thirteenth century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and later in the spectacle making centres in both the Netherlands and Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Spectacle makers created improved types of lenses for the correction of vision based more on empirical knowledge gained from observing the effects of the lenses rather than using the rudimentary optical theory of the day (theory which for the most part could not even adequately explain how spectacles worked).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This practical development, mastery, and experimentation with lenses led directly to the invention of the compound <a href="/wiki/Optical_microscope" title="Optical microscope">optical microscope</a> around 1595, and the <a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">refracting telescope</a> in 1608, both of which appeared in the spectacle making centres in the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LZZginzib4C_page_55_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-LZZginzib4C_page_55-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 17th century <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> expanded on geometric optics in his writings, covering lenses, reflection by flat and curved mirrors, the principles of <a href="/wiki/Pinhole_camera" title="Pinhole camera">pinhole cameras</a>, inverse-square law governing the intensity of light, and the optical explanations of astronomical phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipses</a> and astronomical <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a>. He was also able to correctly deduce the role of the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> as the actual organ that recorded images, finally being able to scientifically quantify the effects of different types of lenses that spectacle makers had been observing over the previous 300 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> After the invention of the telescope Kepler set out the theoretical basis on how they worked and described an improved version, known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keplerian_telescope" title="Keplerian telescope">Keplerian telescope</a></i>, using two convex lenses to produce higher magnification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opticks.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="738" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Opticks.jpg/170px-Opticks.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Opticks.jpg/255px-Opticks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Opticks.jpg/340px-Opticks.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opticks.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cover of the first edition of Newton's <i>Opticks</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Optical theory progressed in the mid-17th century with <a href="/wiki/The_World_(Descartes)#Cartesian_theory_on_light" title="The World (Descartes)">treatises</a> written by philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, which explained a variety of optical phenomena including reflection and refraction by assuming that light was emitted by objects which produced it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabra_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sabra-30">[30]</a></sup> This differed substantively from the ancient Greek emission theory. In the late 1660s and early 1670s, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> expanded Descartes' ideas into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corpuscle_theory_of_light" title="Corpuscle theory of light">corpuscle theory of light</a>, famously determining that white light was a mix of colours which can be separated into its component parts with a <a href="/wiki/Prism" title="Prism">prism</a>. In 1690, <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> proposed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_theory" title="Wave theory">wave theory</a> for light based on suggestions that had been made by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> in 1664. Hooke himself publicly criticised Newton's theories of light and the feud between the two lasted until Hooke's death. In 1704, Newton published <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i> and, at the time, partly because of his success in other areas of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, he was generally considered to be the victor in the debate over the nature of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabra_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sabra-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Newtonian optics was generally accepted until the early 19th century when <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Thomas Young</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustin-Jean_Fresnel" title="Augustin-Jean Fresnel">Augustin-Jean Fresnel</a> conducted experiments on the <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> of light that firmly established light's wave nature. Young's famous <a href="/wiki/Young%27s_interference_experiment" title="Young's interference experiment">double slit experiment</a> showed that light followed the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_superposition" title="Law of superposition">law of superposition</a>, which is a wave-like property not predicted by Newton's corpuscle theory. This work led to a theory of diffraction for light and opened an entire area of study in physical optics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Wave optics was successfully unified with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_theory" title="Electromagnetic theory">electromagnetic theory</a> by <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> in the 1860s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The next development in optical theory came in 1899 when <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a> correctly modelled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody_radiation" title="Blackbody radiation">blackbody radiation</a> by assuming that the exchange of energy between light and matter only occurred in discrete amounts he called <i>quanta</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In 1905 <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> published the theory of the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a> that firmly established the quantization of light itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnnPhysik322132_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnnPhysik322132-35">[35]</a></sup> In 1913 <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> showed that atoms could only emit discrete amounts of energy, thus explaining the discrete lines seen in <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">emission</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_spectrum" title="Absorption spectrum">absorption spectra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The understanding of the interaction between light and matter which followed from these developments not only formed the basis of quantum optics but also was crucial for the <a href="/wiki/History_of_quantum_mechanics" title="History of quantum mechanics">development</a> of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> as a whole. The ultimate culmination, the theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a>, explains all optics and electromagnetic processes in general as the result of the exchange of real and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_particles" title="Virtual particles">virtual</a> <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Quantum optics gained practical importance with the inventions of the <a href="/wiki/Maser" title="Maser">maser</a> in 1953 and of the laser in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Following the work of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dirac" title="Paul Dirac">Paul Dirac</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Sudarshan" title="George Sudarshan">George Sudarshan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_J._Glauber" title="Roy J. Glauber">Roy J. Glauber</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Mandel" title="Leonard Mandel">Leonard Mandel</a> applied quantum theory to the electromagnetic field in the 1950s and 1960s to gain a more detailed understanding of photodetection and the <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistics</a> of light.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_optics">Classical optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Classical optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Classical optics is divided into two main branches: geometrical (or ray) optics and physical (or wave) optics. In geometrical optics, light is considered to travel in straight lines, while in physical optics, light is considered as an electromagnetic wave.</p>
<p>Geometrical optics can be viewed as an approximation of physical optics that applies when the wavelength of the light used is much smaller than the size of the optical elements in the system being modelled.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometrical_optics">Geometrical optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geometrical optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geometrical_optics" title="Geometrical optics">Geometrical optics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reflection_and_refraction.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="641" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Reflection_and_refraction.svg/250px-Reflection_and_refraction.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Reflection_and_refraction.svg/375px-Reflection_and_refraction.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Reflection_and_refraction.svg/500px-Reflection_and_refraction.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reflection_and_refraction.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Geometry of reflection and refraction of light rays</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Geometrical optics</i>, or <i>ray optics</i>, describes the <a href="/wiki/Wave_propagation" title="Wave propagation">propagation</a> of light in terms of "rays" which travel in straight lines, and whose paths are governed by the laws of reflection and refraction at interfaces between different media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> These laws were discovered empirically as far back as 984 AD<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j1_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-j1-11">[11]</a></sup> and have been used in the design of optical components and instruments from then until the present day. They can be summarised as follows:</p>
<p>When a ray of light hits the boundary between two transparent materials, it is divided into a reflected and a refracted ray.</p>
<dl>
<dd>The law of reflection says that the reflected ray lies in the plane of incidence, and the angle of reflection equals the angle of incidence.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>The law of refraction says that the refracted ray lies in the plane of incidence, and the sine of the angle of refraction divided by the sine of the angle of incidence is a constant:</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msub>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msub>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\frac {\sin {\theta _{1}}}{\sin {\theta _{2}}}}=n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7250588a7d37e64d4efae6e0f4b33634ce767f8b" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:10.787ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span>,</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> is a constant for any two materials and a given colour of light. If the first material is air or vacuum, <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of the second material.</p>
<p>The laws of reflection and refraction can be derived from <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_principle" title="Fermat's principle">Fermat's principle</a> which states that <i>the path taken between two points by a ray of light is the path that can be traversed in the least time.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Approximations">Approximations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Approximations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Geometric optics is often simplified by making the <a href="/wiki/Paraxial_approximation" title="Paraxial approximation">paraxial approximation</a>, or "small angle approximation". The mathematical behaviour then becomes linear, allowing optical components and systems to be described by simple matrices. This leads to the techniques of <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_optics" title="Gaussian optics">Gaussian optics</a> and <i>paraxial <a href="/wiki/Ray_tracing_(physics)" title="Ray tracing (physics)">ray tracing</a></i>, which are used to find basic properties of optical systems, such as approximate <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">image</a> and object positions and <a href="/wiki/Magnification" title="Magnification">magnifications</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reflections">Reflections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reflections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">Reflection (physics)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reflection_angles.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="204" data-file-width="170" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Reflection_angles.svg/170px-Reflection_angles.svg.png" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">Diagram of specular reflection</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reflections can be divided into two types: <a href="/wiki/Specular_reflection" title="Specular reflection">specular reflection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_reflection" title="Diffuse reflection">diffuse reflection</a>. Specular reflection describes the gloss of surfaces such as mirrors, which reflect light in a simple, predictable way. This allows for production of reflected images that can be associated with an actual (<a href="/wiki/Real_image" title="Real image">real</a>) or extrapolated (<a href="/wiki/Virtual_image" title="Virtual image">virtual</a>) location in space. Diffuse reflection describes non-glossy materials, such as paper or rock. The reflections from these surfaces can only be described statistically, with the exact distribution of the reflected light depending on the microscopic structure of the material. Many diffuse reflectors are described or can be approximated by <a href="/wiki/Lambert%27s_cosine_law" title="Lambert's cosine law">Lambert's cosine law</a>, which describes surfaces that have equal <a href="/wiki/Luminance" title="Luminance">luminance</a> when viewed from any angle. Glossy surfaces can give both specular and diffuse reflection.</p>
<p>In specular reflection, the direction of the reflected ray is determined by the angle the incident ray makes with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_normal" title="Surface normal">surface normal</a>, a line perpendicular to the surface at the point where the ray hits. The incident and reflected rays and the normal lie in a single plane, and the angle between the reflected ray and the surface normal is the same as that between the incident ray and the normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup> This is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_Reflection" title="Law of Reflection">Law of Reflection</a>.</p>
<p>For <a href="/wiki/Plane_mirror" title="Plane mirror">flat mirrors</a>, the law of reflection implies that images of objects are upright and the same distance behind the mirror as the objects are in front of the mirror. The image size is the same as the object size. The law also implies that <a href="/wiki/Mirror_image" title="Mirror image">mirror images</a> are <a href="/wiki/Parity_(physics)" title="Parity (physics)">parity inverted</a>, which we perceive as a left-right inversion. Images formed from reflection in two (or any even number of) mirrors are not parity inverted. <a href="/wiki/Corner_reflector" title="Corner reflector">Corner reflectors</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Retroreflector" title="Retroreflector">retroreflect</a> light, producing reflected rays that travel back in the direction from which the incident rays came.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Curved_mirror" title="Curved mirror">Mirrors with curved surfaces</a> can be modelled by <a href="/wiki/Ray_tracing_(physics)" title="Ray tracing (physics)">ray-tracing</a> and using the law of reflection at each point on the surface. For <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">mirrors with parabolic surfaces</a>, parallel rays incident on the mirror produce reflected rays that converge at a common <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focus</a>. Other curved surfaces may also focus light, but with aberrations due to the diverging shape causing the focus to be smeared out in space. In particular, spherical mirrors exhibit <a href="/wiki/Spherical_aberration" title="Spherical aberration">spherical aberration</a>. Curved mirrors can form images with magnification greater than or less than one, and the magnification can be negative, indicating that the image is inverted. An upright image formed by reflection in a mirror is always virtual, while an inverted image is real and can be projected onto a screen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Refractions">Refractions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Refractions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">Refraction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snells_law.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="641" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Snells_law.svg/300px-Snells_law.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Snells_law.svg/450px-Snells_law.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Snells_law.svg/600px-Snells_law.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snells_law.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of Snell's Law for the case n<sub>1</sub> &lt; n<sub>2</sub>, such as air/water interface</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Refraction occurs when light travels through an area of space that has a changing index of refraction; this principle allows for lenses and the focusing of light. The simplest case of refraction occurs when there is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interface_(chemistry)" title="Interface (chemistry)">interface</a> between a uniform medium with index of refraction <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n_{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="n_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ee784b70e772f55ede5e6e0bdc929994bff63413" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.467ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> and another medium with index of refraction <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n_{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="n_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/840e456e3058bc0be28e5cf653b170cdbfcc3be4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.467ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>. In such situations, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snell%27s_Law" title="Snell's Law">Snell's Law</a> describes the resulting deflection of the light ray:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</msub>

<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<msub>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mtext> </mtext>
</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n_{1}\sin \theta _{1}=n_{2}\sin \theta _{2}\ }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="n_{1}\sin \theta _{1}=n_{2}\sin \theta _{2}\ " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a02ad752ea6ccadf7a192f943d0529371f6dd0f2" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:20.271ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \theta _{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\theta _{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7f84b9443d095623e02fd287cd095123d70b0278" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.163ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> and <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</mrow>
</msub>

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \theta _{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\theta _{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ed6ea624b20b153403979ffaf5434fc36de2990" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.163ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> are the angles between the normal (to the interface) and the incident and refracted waves, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The index of refraction of a medium is related to the speed, <span class="texhtml"><i>v</i></span>, of light in that medium by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>v</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n=c/v}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle n=c/v}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38116b5eb6dd063ea2413f0ca75d5b02a4035fac" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.842ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>,</dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speed_of_light_in_vacuum" title="Speed of light in vacuum">speed of light in vacuum</a>.</p>
<p>Snell's Law can be used to predict the deflection of light rays as they pass through linear media as long as the indexes of refraction and the geometry of the media are known. For example, the propagation of light through a prism results in the light ray being deflected depending on the shape and orientation of the prism. In most materials, the index of refraction varies with the frequency of the light. Taking this into account, Snell's Law can be used to predict how a prism will disperse light into a spectrum. The discovery of this phenomenon when passing light through a prism is famously attributed to Isaac Newton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some media have an index of refraction which varies gradually with position and, thus, light rays in the medium are curved. This effect is responsible for <a href="/wiki/Mirage" title="Mirage">mirages</a> seen on hot days: a change in index of refraction air with height causes light rays to bend, creating the appearance of specular reflections in the distance (as if on the surface of a pool of water). Optical materials with varying index of refraction are called gradient-index (GRIN) materials. Such materials are used to make <a href="/wiki/Gradient-index_optics" title="Gradient-index optics">gradient-index optics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>For light rays travelling from a material with a high index of refraction to a material with a low index of refraction, Snell's law predicts that there is no <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \theta _{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\theta _{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ed6ea624b20b153403979ffaf5434fc36de2990" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.163ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> when <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \theta _{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\theta _{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7f84b9443d095623e02fd287cd095123d70b0278" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.163ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is large. In this case, no transmission occurs; all the light is reflected. This phenomenon is called <a href="/wiki/Total_internal_reflection" title="Total internal reflection">total internal reflection</a> and allows for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibre_optics" title="Fibre optics">fibre optics</a> technology. As light travels down an optical fibre, it undergoes total internal reflection allowing for essentially no light to be lost over the length of the cable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Lenses">Lenses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Lenses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">Lens (optics)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lens3b.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="362" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Lens3b.svg/360px-Lens3b.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Lens3b.svg/540px-Lens3b.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Lens3b.svg/720px-Lens3b.svg.png 2x" width="360"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lens3b.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A ray tracing diagram for a converging lens.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A device which produces converging or diverging light rays due to refraction is known as a <i>lens</i>. Lenses are characterized by their <a href="/wiki/Focal_length" title="Focal length">focal length</a>: a converging lens has positive focal length, while a diverging lens has negative focal length. Smaller focal length indicates that the lens has a stronger converging or diverging effect. The focal length of a simple lens in air is given by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lensmaker%27s_equation" title="Lensmaker's equation">lensmaker's equation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ray tracing can be used to show how images are formed by a lens. For a <a href="/wiki/Thin_lens" title="Thin lens">thin lens</a> in air, the location of the image is given by the simple equation</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<msub>
<mi>S</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<msub>
<mi>S</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mi>f</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{S_{1}}}+{\frac {1}{S_{2}}}={\frac {1}{f}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\frac {1}{S_{1}}}+{\frac {1}{S_{2}}}={\frac {1}{f}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3d5471b983b47c062376f96c793d2a5fb8b04761" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:14.751ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span>,</dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S_{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5bf84e7fd4fb8259a9b37f956afdf83ee2a020f9" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.497ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the distance from the object to the lens, <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S_{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1143e284d5f25cef778ab482edf6617a523ddd9f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.497ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the distance from the lens to the image, and <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/132e57acb643253e7810ee9702d9581f159a1c61" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.289ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the focal length of the lens. In the <a href="/wiki/Sign_convention" title="Sign convention">sign convention</a> used here, the object and image distances are positive if the object and image are on opposite sides of the lens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lens1.svg"><img alt="Lens1.svg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="345" data-file-width="522" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Lens1.svg/360px-Lens1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Lens1.svg/540px-Lens1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Lens1.svg/720px-Lens1.svg.png 2x" width="360"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lens1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Incoming parallel rays are focused by a converging lens onto a spot one focal length from the lens, on the far side of the lens. This is called the rear <a href="/wiki/Focus_(optics)" title="Focus (optics)">focal point</a> of the lens. Rays from an object at finite distance are focused further from the lens than the focal distance; the closer the object is to the lens, the further the image is from the lens.</p>
<p>With diverging lenses, incoming parallel rays diverge after going through the lens, in such a way that they seem to have originated at a spot one focal length in front of the lens. This is the lens's front focal point. Rays from an object at finite distance are associated with a virtual image that is closer to the lens than the focal point, and on the same side of the lens as the object. The closer the object is to the lens, the closer the virtual image is to the lens. As with mirrors, upright images produced by a single lens are virtual, while inverted images are real.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lenses suffer from <a href="/wiki/Optical_aberration" title="Optical aberration">aberrations</a> that distort images. <i>Monochromatic aberrations</i> occur because the geometry of the lens does not perfectly direct rays from each object point to a single point on the image, while <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_aberration" title="Chromatic aberration">chromatic aberration</a> occurs because the index of refraction of the lens varies with the wavelength of the light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-7"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thin_lens_images.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="293" data-file-width="512" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Thin_lens_images.svg/500px-Thin_lens_images.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Thin_lens_images.svg/750px-Thin_lens_images.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Thin_lens_images.svg/1000px-Thin_lens_images.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thin_lens_images.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Images of black letters in a thin convex lens of focal length <i>f</i>  are shown in red. Selected rays are shown for letters <b>E</b>, <b>I</b> and <b>K</b> in blue, green and orange, respectively. Note that <b>E</b> (at 2<i>f</i>) has an equal-size, real and inverted image; <b>I</b> (at <i>f</i>) has its image at infinity; and <b>K</b> (at <i>f</i>/2) has a double-size, virtual and upright image.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_optics">Physical optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Physical optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physical_optics" title="Physical optics">Physical optics</a></div>
<p>In physical optics, light is considered to propagate as a <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">wave</a>. This model predicts phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a>, which are not explained by geometric optics. The <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> waves in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a> is approximately 3.0×10<sup>8</sup> m/s (exactly 299,792,458 m/s in <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of visible light waves varies between 400 and 700 nm, but the term "light" is also often applied to <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> (0.7–300 μm) and <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation (10–400 nm).</p>
<p>The wave model can be used to make predictions about how an optical system will behave without requiring an explanation of what is "waving" in what medium. Until the middle of the 19th century, most physicists believed in an "ethereal" medium in which the light disturbance propagated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The existence of electromagnetic waves was predicted in 1865 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_waves#derivation" title="Electromagnetic waves">Maxwell's equations</a>. These waves propagate at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> and have varying electric and magnetic fields which are orthogonal to one another, and also to the direction of propagation of the waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Light waves are now generally treated as electromagnetic waves except when <a href="/wiki/Optics#Modern_optics" title="Optics">quantum mechanical effects</a> have to be considered.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modelling_and_design_of_optical_systems_using_physical_optics">Modelling and design of optical systems using physical optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Modelling and design of optical systems using physical optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many simplified approximations are available for analysing and designing optical systems. Most of these use a single <a href="/wiki/Scalar_(physics)" title="Scalar (physics)">scalar</a> quantity to represent the electric field of the light wave, rather than using a <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector" title="Euclidean vector">vector</a> model with orthogonal electric and magnetic vectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Born_and_Wolf_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Born_and_Wolf-47">[47]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Huygens%E2%80%93Fresnel_principle" title="Huygens–Fresnel principle">Huygens–Fresnel</a> equation is one such model. This was derived empirically by Fresnel in 1815, based on Huygens' hypothesis that each point on a wavefront generates a secondary spherical wavefront, which Fresnel combined with the principle of <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superposition</a> of waves. The <a href="/wiki/Kirchhoff%27s_diffraction_formula" title="Kirchhoff's diffraction formula">Kirchhoff diffraction equation</a>, which is derived using Maxwell's equations, puts the Huygens-Fresnel equation on a firmer physical foundation. Examples of the application of Huygens–Fresnel principle can be found in the sections on <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_diffraction_(mathematics)" title="Fraunhofer diffraction (mathematics)">Fraunhofer diffraction</a>.</p>
<p>More rigorous models, involving the modelling of both electric and magnetic fields of the light wave, are required when dealing with the detailed interaction of light with materials where the interaction depends on their electric and magnetic properties. For instance, the behaviour of a light wave interacting with a metal surface is quite different from what happens when it interacts with a dielectric material. A vector model must also be used to model polarised light.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">Numerical modeling</a> techniques such as the <a href="/wiki/Finite_element_method" title="Finite element method">finite element method</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Boundary_element_method" title="Boundary element method">boundary element method</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Transmission-line_matrix_method" title="Transmission-line matrix method">transmission-line matrix method</a> can be used to model the propagation of light in systems which cannot be solved analytically. Such models are computationally demanding and are normally only used to solve small-scale problems that require accuracy beyond that which can be achieved with analytical solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>All of the results from geometrical optics can be recovered using the techniques of <a href="/wiki/Fourier_optics" title="Fourier optics">Fourier optics</a> which apply many of the same mathematical and analytical techniques used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acoustic_engineering" title="Acoustic engineering">acoustic engineering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Signal_processing" title="Signal processing">signal processing</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gaussian_beam" title="Gaussian beam">Gaussian beam propagation</a> is a simple paraxial physical optics model for the propagation of coherent radiation such as laser beams. This technique partially accounts for diffraction, allowing accurate calculations of the rate at which a laser beam expands with distance, and the minimum size to which the beam can be focused. Gaussian beam propagation thus bridges the gap between geometric and physical optics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Superposition_and_interference">Superposition and interference</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Superposition and interference">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">Superposition principle</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(optics)" title="Interference (optics)">Interference (optics)</a></div>
<p>In the absence of <a href="/wiki/Nonlinear_optics" title="Nonlinear optics">nonlinear</a> effects, the <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superposition principle</a> can be used to predict the shape of interacting waveforms through the simple addition of the disturbances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interference_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-interference-50">[50]</a></sup> This interaction of waves to produce a resulting pattern is generally termed "interference" and can result in a variety of outcomes. If two waves of the same wavelength and frequency are <i>in <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)" title="Phase (waves)">phase</a></i>, both the wave crests and wave troughs align. This results in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constructive_interference" title="Constructive interference">constructive interference</a> and an increase in the amplitude of the wave, which for light is associated with a brightening of the waveform in that location. Alternatively, if the two waves of the same wavelength and frequency are out of phase, then the wave crests will align with wave troughs and vice versa. This results in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Destructive_interference" title="Destructive interference">destructive interference</a> and a decrease in the amplitude of the wave, which for light is associated with a dimming of the waveform at that location. See below for an illustration of this effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interference_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-interference-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<table>
<tr>
<td><b>combined<br/>
waveform</b></td>
<td colspan="2" rowspan="3"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interference_of_two_waves.svg"><img alt="Interference of two waves.svg" data-file-height="122" data-file-width="365" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Interference_of_two_waves.svg/365px-Interference_of_two_waves.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Interference_of_two_waves.svg/548px-Interference_of_two_waves.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Interference_of_two_waves.svg/730px-Interference_of_two_waves.svg.png 2x" width="365"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>wave 1</b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>wave 2</b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><br/></td>

of phase</b></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dieselrainbow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="871" data-file-width="976" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/300px-Dieselrainbow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/450px-Dieselrainbow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/600px-Dieselrainbow.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dieselrainbow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
When oil or fuel is spilled, colourful patterns are formed by thin-film interference.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Huygens%E2%80%93Fresnel_principle" title="Huygens–Fresnel principle">Huygens–Fresnel principle</a> states that every point of a wavefront is associated with the production of a new disturbance, it is possible for a wavefront to interfere with itself constructively or destructively at different locations producing bright and dark fringes in regular and predictable patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interference_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-interference-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Interferometry" title="Interferometry">Interferometry</a> is the science of measuring these patterns, usually as a means of making precise determinations of distances or <a href="/wiki/Angular_resolution" title="Angular resolution">angular resolutions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interferometry_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-interferometry-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Michelson_interferometer" title="Michelson interferometer">Michelson interferometer</a> was a famous instrument which used interference effects to accurately measure the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The appearance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thin_film_optics" title="Thin film optics">thin films and coatings</a> is directly affected by interference effects. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antireflective_coating" title="Antireflective coating">Antireflective coatings</a> use destructive interference to reduce the reflectivity of the surfaces they coat, and can be used to minimise glare and unwanted reflections. The simplest case is a single layer with thickness one-fourth the wavelength of incident light. The reflected wave from the top of the film and the reflected wave from the film/material interface are then exactly 180° out of phase, causing destructive interference. The waves are only exactly out of phase for one wavelength, which would typically be chosen to be near the centre of the visible spectrum, around 550 nm. More complex designs using multiple layers can achieve low reflectivity over a broad band, or extremely low reflectivity at a single wavelength.</p>
<p>Constructive interference in thin films can create strong reflection of light in a range of wavelengths, which can be narrow or broad depending on the design of the coating. These films are used to make <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_mirror" title="Dielectric mirror">dielectric mirrors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interference_filter" title="Interference filter">interference filters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_reflector" title="Heat reflector">heat reflectors</a>, and filters for colour separation in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_television" title="Colour television">colour television</a> cameras. This interference effect is also what causes the colourful rainbow patterns seen in oil slicks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interference_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-interference-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diffraction_and_optical_resolution">Diffraction and optical resolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Diffraction and optical resolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">Diffraction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optical_resolution" title="Optical resolution">Optical resolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Double_slit_diffraction.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="265" data-file-width="500" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Double_slit_diffraction.svg/300px-Double_slit_diffraction.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Double_slit_diffraction.svg/450px-Double_slit_diffraction.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Double_slit_diffraction.svg/600px-Double_slit_diffraction.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>

These fringes are separated by angle <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.405ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Diffraction is the process by which light interference is most commonly observed. The effect was first described in 1665 by <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Maria_Grimaldi" title="Francesco Maria Grimaldi">Francesco Maria Grimaldi</a>, who also coined the term from the Latin <i>diffringere</i>, 'to break into pieces'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Later that century, Robert Hooke and Isaac Newton also described phenomena now known to be diffraction in <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_rings" title="Newton's rings">Newton's rings</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Gregory_(astronomer_and_mathematician)" title="James Gregory (astronomer and mathematician)">James Gregory</a> recorded his observations of diffraction patterns from bird feathers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first physical optics model of diffraction that relied on the <a href="/wiki/Huygens%E2%80%93Fresnel_principle" title="Huygens–Fresnel principle">Huygens–Fresnel principle</a> was developed in 1803 by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Thomas Young</a> in <a href="/wiki/Young%27s_interference_experiment" title="Young's interference experiment">his interference experiments</a> with the interference patterns of two closely spaced slits. Young showed that his results could only be explained if the two slits acted as two unique sources of waves rather than corpuscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In 1815 and 1818, Augustin-Jean Fresnel firmly established the mathematics of how wave interference can account for diffraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The simplest physical models of diffraction use equations that describe the angular separation of light and dark fringes due to light of a particular wavelength (λ). In general, the equation takes the form</p>

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mi>d</mi>

</dl>

<p>This equation is modified slightly to take into account a variety of situations such as diffraction through a single gap, diffraction through multiple slits, or diffraction through a <a href="/wiki/Diffraction_grating" title="Diffraction grating">diffraction grating</a> that contains a large number of slits at equal spacing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diffraction_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-diffraction-58">[58]</a></sup> More complicated models of diffraction require working with the mathematics of <a href="/wiki/Fresnel_diffraction" title="Fresnel diffraction">Fresnel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_diffraction" title="Fraunhofer diffraction">Fraunhofer diffraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phyoptics_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-phyoptics-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a> makes use of the fact that atoms in a <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystal</a> have regular spacing at distances that are on the order of one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angstrom" title="Angstrom">angstrom</a>. To see diffraction patterns, x-rays with similar wavelengths to that spacing are passed through the crystal. Since crystals are three-dimensional objects rather than two-dimensional gratings, the associated diffraction pattern varies in two directions according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bragg_reflection" title="Bragg reflection">Bragg reflection</a>, with the associated bright spots occurring in <a href="/wiki/Diffraction_topography" title="Diffraction topography">unique patterns</a> and <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>d</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle d}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="d" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e85ff03cbe0c7341af6b982e47e9f90d235c66ab" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.226ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> being twice the spacing between atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diffraction_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-diffraction-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Diffraction effects limit the ability for an optical detector to <a href="/wiki/Optical_resolution" title="Optical resolution">optically resolve</a> separate light sources. In general, light that is passing through an <a href="/wiki/Aperture" title="Aperture">aperture</a> will experience diffraction and the best images that can be created (as described in <a href="/wiki/Diffraction-limited_system" title="Diffraction-limited system">diffraction-limited optics</a>) appear as a central spot with surrounding bright rings, separated by dark nulls; this pattern is known as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airy_pattern" title="Airy pattern">Airy pattern</a>, and the central bright lobe as an <a href="/wiki/Airy_disk" title="Airy disk">Airy disk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup> The size of such a disk is given by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
