<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1.22</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mi>D</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \sin \theta =1.22{\frac {\lambda }{D}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt=" \sin \theta = 1.22 \frac{\lambda}{D}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/af4709f32fba83bc3a5bed37b719af6c1ce3a04d" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:14.431ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>θ</i> is the angular resolution, <i>λ</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of the light, and <i>D</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a> of the lens aperture. If the angular separation of the two points is significantly less than the Airy disk angular radius, then the two points cannot be resolved in the image, but if their angular separation is much greater than this, distinct images of the two points are formed and they can therefore be resolved. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Strutt,_3rd_Baron_Rayleigh" title="John Strutt, 3rd Baron Rayleigh">Rayleigh</a> defined the somewhat arbitrary "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rayleigh_criterion" title="Rayleigh criterion">Rayleigh criterion</a>" that two points whose angular separation is equal to the Airy disk radius (measured to first null, that is, to the first place where no light is seen) can be considered to be resolved. It can be seen that the greater the diameter of the lens or its aperture, the finer the resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diffraction_58-3"><a href="#cite_note-diffraction-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_interferometer" title="Astronomical interferometer">Interferometry</a>, with its ability to mimic extremely large baseline apertures, allows for the greatest angular resolution possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interferometry_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-interferometry-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>For astronomical imaging, the atmosphere prevents optimal resolution from being achieved in the visible spectrum due to the atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scattering</a> and dispersion which cause stars to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scintillation_(astronomy)" title="Scintillation (astronomy)">twinkle</a>. Astronomers refer to this effect as the quality of <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_seeing" title="Astronomical seeing">astronomical seeing</a>. Techniques known as <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> have been used to eliminate the atmospheric disruption of images and achieve results that approach the diffraction limit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersion_and_scattering">Dispersion and scattering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Dispersion and scattering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">Dispersion (optics)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">Scattering</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/220px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/330px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/440px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Conceptual animation of light dispersion through a prism. High frequency (blue) light is deflected the most, and low frequency (red) the least.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Refractive processes take place in the physical optics limit, where the wavelength of light is similar to other distances, as a kind of scattering. The simplest type of scattering is <a href="/wiki/Thomson_scattering" title="Thomson scattering">Thomson scattering</a> which occurs when electromagnetic waves are deflected by single particles. In the limit of Thomson scattering, in which the wavelike nature of light is evident, light is dispersed independent of the frequency, in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Compton_scattering" title="Compton scattering">Compton scattering</a> which is frequency-dependent and strictly a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanical" title="Quantum mechanical">quantum mechanical</a> process, involving the nature of light as particles. In a statistical sense, elastic scattering of light by numerous particles much smaller than the wavelength of the light is a process known as <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a> while the similar process for scattering by particles that are similar or larger in wavelength is known as <a href="/wiki/Mie_scattering" title="Mie scattering">Mie scattering</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Tyndall_effect" title="Tyndall effect">Tyndall effect</a> being a commonly observed result. A small proportion of light scattering from atoms or molecules may undergo <a href="/wiki/Raman_scattering" title="Raman scattering">Raman scattering</a>, wherein the frequency changes due to excitation of the atoms and molecules. <a href="/wiki/Brillouin_scattering" title="Brillouin scattering">Brillouin scattering</a> occurs when the frequency of light changes due to local changes with time and movements of a dense material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dispersion occurs when different frequencies of light have different <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocities</a>, due either to material properties (<i>material dispersion</i>) or to the geometry of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_waveguide" title="Optical waveguide">optical waveguide</a> (<i>waveguide dispersion</i>). The most familiar form of dispersion is a decrease in index of refraction with increasing wavelength, which is seen in most transparent materials. This is called "normal dispersion". It occurs in all <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric materials</a>, in wavelength ranges where the material does not absorb light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J286_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-J286-62">[62]</a></sup> In wavelength ranges where a medium has significant absorption, the index of refraction can increase with wavelength. This is called "anomalous dispersion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-8"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-J286_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-J286-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The separation of colours by a prism is an example of normal dispersion. At the surfaces of the prism, Snell's law predicts that light incident at an angle θ to the normal will be refracted at an angle arcsin(sin (θ) / <i>n</i>). Thus, blue light, with its higher refractive index, is bent more strongly than red light, resulting in the well-known <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbow</a> pattern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoptics_42-9"><a href="#cite_note-Geoptics-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:468px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_group.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="33" data-file-width="466" height="33" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/Wave_group.gif" width="466"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">Dispersion: two sinusoids propagating at different speeds make a moving interference pattern. The red dot moves with the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a>, and the green dots propagate with the <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">group velocity</a>. In this case, the phase velocity is twice the group velocity. The red dot overtakes two green dots, when moving from the left to the right of the figure. In effect, the individual waves (which travel with the phase velocity) escape from the wave packet (which travels with the group velocity).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Material dispersion is often characterised by the <a href="/wiki/Abbe_number" title="Abbe number">Abbe number</a>, which gives a simple measure of dispersion based on the index of refraction at three specific wavelengths. Waveguide dispersion is dependent on the <a href="/wiki/Propagation_constant" title="Propagation constant">propagation constant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup> Both kinds of dispersion cause changes in the group characteristics of the wave, the features of the wave packet that change with the same frequency as the amplitude of the electromagnetic wave. "Group velocity dispersion" manifests as a spreading-out of the signal "envelope" of the radiation and can be quantified with a group dispersion delay parameter:</p>

<p>where <i>n</i> is the index of refraction and <i>c</i> is the speed of light in a vacuum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> This gives a simpler form for the dispersion delay parameter:</p>

</dl>
<p>If <i>D</i> is less than zero, the medium is said to have <i>positive dispersion</i> or normal dispersion. If <i>D</i> is greater than zero, the medium has <i>negative dispersion</i>. If a light pulse is propagated through a normally dispersive medium, the result is the higher frequency components slow down more than the lower frequency components. The pulse therefore becomes <i>positively <a href="/wiki/Chirp" title="Chirp">chirped</a></i>, or <i>up-chirped</i>, increasing in frequency with time. This causes the spectrum coming out of a prism to appear with red light the least refracted and blue/violet light the most refracted. Conversely, if a pulse travels through an anomalously (negatively) dispersive medium, high frequency components travel faster than the lower ones, and the pulse becomes <i>negatively chirped</i>, or <i>down-chirped</i>, decreasing in frequency with time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The result of group velocity dispersion, whether negative or positive, is ultimately temporal spreading of the pulse. This makes dispersion management extremely important in optical communications systems based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_fibre" title="Optical fibre">optical fibres</a>, since if dispersion is too high, a group of pulses representing information will each spread in time and merge, making it impossible to extract the signal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-optnet_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-optnet-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization">Polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">Polarization (waves)</a></div>
<p>Polarization is a general property of waves that describes the orientation of their oscillations. For <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse waves</a> such as many electromagnetic waves, it describes the orientation of the oscillations in the plane perpendicular to the wave's direction of travel. The oscillations may be oriented in a single direction (<a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">linear polarization</a>), or the oscillation direction may rotate as the wave travels (<a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circular</a> or <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_polarization" title="Elliptical polarization">elliptical polarization</a>). Circularly polarised waves can rotate rightward or leftward in the direction of travel, and which of those two rotations is present in a wave is called the wave's <a href="/wiki/Polarimetry" title="Polarimetry">chirality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-light_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-light-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The typical way to consider polarization is to keep track of the orientation of the electric field <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometry)" title="Vector (geometry)">vector</a> as the electromagnetic wave propagates. The electric field vector of a plane wave may be arbitrarily divided into two perpendicular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_components" title="Vector components">components</a> labeled <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> (with <b>z</b> indicating the direction of travel). The shape traced out in the x-y plane by the electric field vector is a <a href="/wiki/Lissajous_curve" title="Lissajous curve">Lissajous figure</a> that describes the <i>polarization state</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup> The following figures show some examples of the evolution of the electric field vector (blue), with time (the vertical axes), at a particular point in space, along with its <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> components (red/left and green/right), and the path traced by the vector in the plane (purple): The same evolution would occur when looking at the electric field at a particular time while evolving the point in space, along the direction opposite to propagation.</p>
<div style="float:left;width:170px">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_(Linear).svg" title="Linear polarization diagram"><img alt="Linear polarization diagram" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="250" height="625" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Polarisation_%28Linear%29.svg/250px-Polarisation_%28Linear%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Polarisation_%28Linear%29.svg/375px-Polarisation_%28Linear%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Polarisation_%28Linear%29.svg/500px-Polarisation_%28Linear%29.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
</div>
<center><i>Linear</i></center>
</div>
<div style="float:left;width:170px">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_(Circular).svg" title="Circular polarization diagram"><img alt="Circular polarization diagram" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="250" height="625" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Polarisation_%28Circular%29.svg/250px-Polarisation_%28Circular%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Polarisation_%28Circular%29.svg/375px-Polarisation_%28Circular%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Polarisation_%28Circular%29.svg/500px-Polarisation_%28Circular%29.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
</div>
<center><i>Circular</i></center>
</div>
<div style="float:left;width:170px">
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarisation_(Elliptical).svg" title="Elliptical polarization diagram"><img alt="Elliptical polarization diagram" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="250" height="625" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Polarisation_%28Elliptical%29.svg/250px-Polarisation_%28Elliptical%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Polarisation_%28Elliptical%29.svg/375px-Polarisation_%28Elliptical%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Polarisation_%28Elliptical%29.svg/500px-Polarisation_%28Elliptical%29.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
</div>
<center><i>Elliptical polarization</i></center>
</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>In the leftmost figure above, the x and y components of the light wave are in phase. In this case, the ratio of their strengths is constant, so the direction of the electric vector (the vector sum of these two components) is constant. Since the tip of the vector traces out a single line in the plane, this special case is called <a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">linear polarization</a>. The direction of this line depends on the relative amplitudes of the two components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-light_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-light-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the middle figure, the two orthogonal components have the same amplitudes and are 90° out of phase. In this case, one component is zero when the other component is at maximum or minimum amplitude. There are two possible phase relationships that satisfy this requirement: the <i>x</i> component can be 90° ahead of the <i>y</i> component or it can be 90° behind the <i>y</i> component. In this special case, the electric vector traces out a circle in the plane, so this polarization is called circular polarization. The rotation direction in the circle depends on which of the two phase relationships exists and corresponds to <i>right-hand circular polarization</i> and <i>left-hand circular polarization</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-6"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In all other cases, where the two components either do not have the same amplitudes and/or their phase difference is neither zero nor a multiple of 90°, the polarization is called <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_polarization" title="Elliptical polarization">elliptical polarization</a> because the electric vector traces out an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> in the plane (the <i>polarization ellipse</i>). This is shown in the above figure on the right. Detailed mathematics of polarization is done using <a href="/wiki/Jones_calculus" title="Jones calculus">Jones calculus</a> and is characterised by the <a href="/wiki/Stokes_parameters" title="Stokes parameters">Stokes parameters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-7"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Changing_polarization">Changing polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Changing polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Media that have different indexes of refraction for different polarization modes are called <i><a href="/wiki/Birefringence" title="Birefringence">birefringent</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-light_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-light-66">[66]</a></sup> Well known manifestations of this effect appear in optical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_plate" title="Wave plate">wave plates</a>/retarders (linear modes) and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faraday_rotation" title="Faraday rotation">Faraday rotation</a>/<a href="/wiki/Optical_rotation" title="Optical rotation">optical rotation</a> (circular modes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-8"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup> If the path length in the birefringent medium is sufficient, plane waves will exit the material with a significantly different propagation direction, due to <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a>. For example, this is the case with macroscopic crystals of <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a>, which present the viewer with two offset, orthogonally polarised images of whatever is viewed through them. It was this effect that provided the first discovery of polarization, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erasmus_Bartholinus" title="Erasmus Bartholinus">Erasmus Bartholinus</a> in 1669. In addition, the phase shift, and thus the change in polarization state, is usually frequency dependent, which, in combination with <a href="/wiki/Dichroism" title="Dichroism">dichroism</a>, often gives rise to bright colours and rainbow-like effects. In <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a>, such properties, known as <a href="/wiki/Pleochroism" title="Pleochroism">pleochroism</a>, are frequently exploited for the purpose of identifying minerals using polarization <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscopes</a>. Additionally, many plastics that are not normally birefringent will become so when subject to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_stress" title="Mechanical stress">mechanical stress</a>, a phenomenon which is the basis of <a href="/wiki/Photoelasticity" title="Photoelasticity">photoelasticity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-light_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-light-66">[66]</a></sup> Non-birefringent methods, to rotate the linear polarization of light beams, include the use of prismatic <a href="/wiki/Polarization_rotator" title="Polarization rotator">polarization rotators</a> which use <a href="/wiki/Total_internal_reflection" title="Total internal reflection">total internal reflection</a> in a prism set designed for efficient collinear transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malus_law.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="159" data-file-width="230" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Malus_law.svg/350px-Malus_law.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Malus_law.svg/525px-Malus_law.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Malus_law.svg/700px-Malus_law.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malus_law.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A polariser changing the orientation of linearly polarised light.<br/>
In this picture, <i>θ</i><sub>1</sub> – <i>θ<sub>0</sub></i> = <i>θ<sub>i</sub></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Media that reduce the amplitude of certain polarization modes are called <i><a href="/wiki/Dichroism" title="Dichroism">dichroic</a></i>, with devices that block nearly all of the radiation in one mode known as <i>polarizing filters</i> or simply "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polariser" title="Polariser">polarisers</a>". Malus' law, which is named after <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne-Louis_Malus" title="Étienne-Louis Malus">Étienne-Louis Malus</a>, says that when a perfect polariser is placed in a linear polarised beam of light, the intensity, <i>I</i>, of the light that passes through is given by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>I</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>I</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<msup>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mspace width="1em"></mspace>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle I=I_{0}\cos ^{2}\theta _{i}\quad ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt=" I = I_0 \cos^2 \theta_i \quad ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/78c08e35384f33bd6d40d587741d744e84264f19" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:16.252ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>I</i><sub>0</sub> is the initial intensity,</dd>
<dd>and <i>θ<sub>i</sub></i> is the angle between the light's initial polarization direction and the axis of the polariser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-light_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-light-66">[66]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>A beam of unpolarised light can be thought of as containing a uniform mixture of linear polarizations at all possible angles. Since the average value of <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \cos ^{2}\theta }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\cos ^{2}\theta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/311894fdc65ae89f6b2e40edac3d3281a0727680" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.692ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> is 1/2, the transmission coefficient becomes</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>I</mi>
<msub>
<mi>I</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mspace width="1em"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {I}{I_{0}}}={\frac {1}{2}}\quad }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt=" \frac {I}{I_0} = \frac {1}{2}\quad" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2641081a0a38b304e67af052ac1e61a98cdb765f" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:10.372ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>In practice, some light is lost in the polariser and the actual transmission of unpolarised light will be somewhat lower than this, around 38% for Polaroid-type polarisers but considerably higher (&gt;49.9%) for some birefringent prism types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-9"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to birefringence and dichroism in extended media, polarization effects can also occur at the (reflective) interface between two materials of different refractive index. These effects are treated by the <a href="/wiki/Fresnel_equations" title="Fresnel equations">Fresnel equations</a>. Part of the wave is transmitted and part is reflected, with the ratio depending on angle of incidence and the angle of refraction. In this way, physical optics recovers <a href="/wiki/Brewster%27s_angle" title="Brewster's angle">Brewster's angle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-10"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup> When light reflects from a <a href="/wiki/Thin-film_optics" title="Thin-film optics">thin film</a> on a surface, interference between the reflections from the film's surfaces can produce polarization in the reflected and transmitted light.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_light">Natural light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Natural light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CircularPolarizer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="946" data-file-width="2484" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CircularPolarizer.jpg/400px-CircularPolarizer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CircularPolarizer.jpg/600px-CircularPolarizer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CircularPolarizer.jpg/800px-CircularPolarizer.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CircularPolarizer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The effects of a <a href="/wiki/Photographic_filter#Polarizer" title="Photographic filter">polarising filter</a> on the sky in a photograph. Left picture is taken without polariser. For the right picture, filter was adjusted to eliminate certain polarizations of the scattered blue light from the sky.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most sources of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> contain a large number of atoms or molecules that emit light. The orientation of the electric fields produced by these emitters may not be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_correlation" title="Statistical correlation">correlated</a>, in which case the light is said to be <i>unpolarised</i>. If there is partial correlation between the emitters, the light is <i>partially polarised</i>. If the polarization is consistent across the spectrum of the source, partially polarised light can be described as a superposition of a completely unpolarised component, and a completely polarised one. One may then describe the light in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_polarization" title="Degree of polarization">degree of polarization</a>, and the parameters of the polarization ellipse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-11"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Light reflected by shiny transparent materials is partly or fully polarised, except when the light is normal (perpendicular) to the surface. It was this effect that allowed the mathematician <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne-Louis_Malus" title="Étienne-Louis Malus">Étienne-Louis Malus</a> to make the measurements that allowed for his development of the first mathematical models for polarised light. Polarization occurs when light is scattered in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. The scattered light produces the brightness and colour in clear <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">skies</a>. This partial polarization of scattered light can be taken advantage of using polarizing filters to darken the sky in <a href="/wiki/Science_of_photography" title="Science of photography">photographs</a>. Optical polarization is principally of importance in <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Circular_dichroism" title="Circular dichroism">circular dichroism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optical_rotation" title="Optical rotation">optical rotation</a> ("<i>circular birefringence</i>") exhibited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_activity" title="Optical activity">optically active</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">chiral</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">molecules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecht_44-12"><a href="#cite_note-hecht-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_optics">Modern optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modern optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Optical_physics" title="Optical physics">Optical physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optical_engineering" title="Optical engineering">Optical engineering</a></div>
<p><i>Modern optics</i> encompasses the areas of optical science and engineering that became popular in the 20th century. These areas of optical science typically relate to the electromagnetic or quantum properties of light but do include other topics. A major subfield of modern optics, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_optics" title="Quantum optics">quantum optics</a>, deals with specifically quantum mechanical properties of light. Quantum optics is not just theoretical; some modern devices, such as lasers, have principles of operation that depend on quantum mechanics. Light detectors, such as <a href="/wiki/Photomultiplier" title="Photomultiplier">photomultipliers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Channeltron" title="Channeltron">channeltrons</a>, respond to individual photons. Electronic <a href="/wiki/Image_sensor" title="Image sensor">image sensors</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCDs</a>, exhibit <a href="/wiki/Shot_noise" title="Shot noise">shot noise</a> corresponding to the statistics of individual photon events. <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">Light-emitting diodes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_cell" title="Photovoltaic cell">photovoltaic cells</a>, too, cannot be understood without quantum mechanics. In the study of these devices, quantum optics often overlaps with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_electronics" title="Quantum electronics">quantum electronics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Specialty areas of optics research include the study of how light interacts with specific materials as in <a href="/wiki/Crystal_optics" title="Crystal optics">crystal optics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metamaterial" title="Metamaterial">metamaterials</a>. Other research focuses on the phenomenology of electromagnetic waves as in <a href="/wiki/Optical_vortex" title="Optical vortex">singular optics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-imaging_optics" title="Non-imaging optics">non-imaging optics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-linear_optics" title="Non-linear optics">non-linear optics</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Statistical_optics&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Statistical optics (page does not exist)">statistical optics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radiometry" title="Radiometry">radiometry</a>. Additionally, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_engineer" title="Computer engineer">computer engineers</a> have taken an interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integrated_optics" title="Integrated optics">integrated optics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machine_vision" title="Machine vision">machine vision</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photonic_computing" title="Photonic computing">photonic computing</a> as possible components of the "next generation" of computers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today, the pure science of optics is called optical science or <a href="/wiki/Optical_physics" title="Optical physics">optical physics</a> to distinguish it from applied optical sciences, which are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Optical_engineering" title="Optical engineering">optical engineering</a>. Prominent subfields of optical engineering include <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">illumination engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photonics" title="Photonics">photonics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Optoelectronics" title="Optoelectronics">optoelectronics</a> with practical applications like <a href="/wiki/Optical_lens_design" title="Optical lens design">lens design</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fabrication_and_testing_(optical_components)" title="Fabrication and testing (optical components)">fabrication and testing of optical components</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Image_processing" title="Image processing">image processing</a>. Some of these fields overlap, with nebulous boundaries between the subjects terms that mean slightly different things in different parts of the world and in different areas of industry. A professional community of researchers in nonlinear optics has developed in the last several decades due to advances in <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser technology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lasers">Lasers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Lasers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">Laser</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Military_laser_experiment.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Military_laser_experiment.jpg/220px-Military_laser_experiment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Military_laser_experiment.jpg/330px-Military_laser_experiment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Military_laser_experiment.jpg/440px-Military_laser_experiment.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Military_laser_experiment.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Experiments such as this one with high-power <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a> are part of the modern optics research.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A laser is a device that emits light (electromagnetic radiation) through a process called <i><a href="/wiki/Stimulated_emission" title="Stimulated emission">stimulated emission</a></i>. The term <i>laser</i> is an <a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym">acronym</a> for <i>Light Amplification by Stimulated Emission of Radiation</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Laser light is usually spatially <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(physics)" title="Coherence (physics)">coherent</a>, which means that the light either is emitted in a narrow, <a href="/wiki/Beam_divergence" title="Beam divergence">low-divergence beam</a>, or can be converted into one with the help of optical components such as <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lenses</a>. Because the <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> equivalent of the laser, the <i>maser</i>, was developed first, devices that emit microwave and <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">radio</a> frequencies are usually called <i>masers</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_VLT%E2%80%99s_Artificial_Star.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2667" data-file-width="4000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/The_VLT%E2%80%99s_Artificial_Star.jpg/220px-The_VLT%E2%80%99s_Artificial_Star.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/The_VLT%E2%80%99s_Artificial_Star.jpg/330px-The_VLT%E2%80%99s_Artificial_Star.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/The_VLT%E2%80%99s_Artificial_Star.jpg/440px-The_VLT%E2%80%99s_Artificial_Star.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_VLT%E2%80%99s_Artificial_Star.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">VLT</a>’s laser guided star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first working laser was demonstrated on 16 May 1960 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodore_Maiman" title="Theodore Maiman">Theodore Maiman</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hughes_Research_Laboratories" title="Hughes Research Laboratories">Hughes Research Laboratories</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> When first invented, they were called "a solution looking for a problem".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Since then, lasers have become a multibillion-dollar industry, finding utility in thousands of highly varied applications. The first application of lasers visible in the daily lives of the general population was the supermarket <a href="/wiki/Barcode" title="Barcode">barcode</a> scanner, introduced in 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laserdisc" title="Laserdisc">laserdisc</a> player, introduced in 1978, was the first successful consumer product to include a laser, but the <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">compact disc</a> player was the first laser-equipped device to become truly common in consumers' homes, beginning in 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC6950933_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC6950933-77">[77]</a></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Optical_storage" title="Optical storage">optical storage</a> devices use a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiconductor_laser" title="Semiconductor laser">semiconductor laser</a> less than a millimetre wide to scan the surface of the disc for data retrieval. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibre-optic_communication" title="Fibre-optic communication">Fibre-optic communication</a> relies on lasers to transmit large amounts of information at the speed of light. Other common applications of lasers include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_printers" title="Laser printers">laser printers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laser_pointer" title="Laser pointer">laser pointers</a>. Lasers are used in medicine in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Bloodless_surgery" title="Bloodless surgery">bloodless surgery</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_eye_surgery" title="Laser eye surgery">laser eye surgery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Laser_capture_microdissection" title="Laser capture microdissection">laser capture microdissection</a> and in military applications such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airborne_Laser" title="Airborne Laser">missile defence systems</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DIRCM" title="DIRCM">electro-optical countermeasures (EOCM)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">lidar</a>. Lasers are also used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holograms" title="Holograms">holograms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bubblegram" title="Bubblegram">bubblegrams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laser_lighting_display" title="Laser lighting display">laser light shows</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Laser_hair_removal" title="Laser hair removal">laser hair removal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kapitsa.E2.80.93Dirac_effect">Kapitsa–Dirac effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Kapitsa–Dirac effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kapitsa%E2%80%93Dirac_effect" title="Kapitsa–Dirac effect">Kapitsa–Dirac effect</a> causes beams of particles to diffract as the result of meeting a standing wave of light. Light can be used to position matter using various phenomena (see <a href="/wiki/Optical_tweezers" title="Optical tweezers">optical tweezers</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Optics is part of everyday life. The ubiquity of <a href="/wiki/Visual_system" title="Visual system">visual systems</a> in biology indicates the central role optics plays as the science of one of the <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">five senses</a>. Many people benefit from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyeglasses" title="Eyeglasses">eyeglasses</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contact_lenses" title="Contact lenses">contact lenses</a>, and optics are integral to the functioning of many consumer goods including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cameras" title="Cameras">cameras</a>. Rainbows and <a href="/wiki/Mirage" title="Mirage">mirages</a> are examples of optical phenomena. <a href="/wiki/Optical_communication" title="Optical communication">Optical communication</a> provides the backbone for both the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> and modern <a href="/wiki/Telephony" title="Telephony">telephony</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_eye">Human eye</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Human eye">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eye-diagram_no_circles_border.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1208" data-file-width="1237" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Eye-diagram_no_circles_border.svg/300px-Eye-diagram_no_circles_border.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Eye-diagram_no_circles_border.svg/450px-Eye-diagram_no_circles_border.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Eye-diagram_no_circles_border.svg/600px-Eye-diagram_no_circles_border.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eye-diagram_no_circles_border.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Model of a human eye. Features mentioned in this article are 3. <a href="/wiki/Ciliary_muscle" title="Ciliary muscle">ciliary muscle</a>, 6. <a href="/wiki/Pupil" title="Pupil">pupil</a>, 8. <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">cornea</a>, 10. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lens_cortex" title="Lens cortex">lens cortex</a>, 22. <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">optic nerve</a>, 26. <a href="/wiki/Fovea_centralis" title="Fovea centralis">fovea</a>, 30. <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">Human eye</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photometry_(optics)" title="Photometry (optics)">Photometry (optics)</a></div>
<p>The human eye functions by focusing light onto a layer of <a href="/wiki/Photoreceptor_cell" title="Photoreceptor cell">photoreceptor cells</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a>, which forms the inner lining of the back of the eye. The focusing is accomplished by a series of transparent media. Light entering the eye passes first through the <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">cornea</a>, which provides much of the eye's optical power. The light then continues through the fluid just behind the cornea—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior_chamber" title="Anterior chamber">anterior chamber</a>, then passes through the <a href="/wiki/Pupil" title="Pupil">pupil</a>. The light then passes through the <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">lens</a>, which focuses the light further and allows adjustment of focus. The light then passes through the main body of fluid in the eye—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitreous_humour" title="Vitreous humour">vitreous humour</a>, and reaches the retina. The cells in the retina line the back of the eye, except for where the <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">optic nerve</a> exits; this results in a <a href="/wiki/Blind_spot_(vision)" title="Blind spot (vision)">blind spot</a>.</p>
<p>There are two types of photoreceptor cells, rods and cones, which are sensitive to different aspects of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eyeoptics_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-eyeoptics-79">[79]</a></sup> Rod cells are sensitive to the intensity of light over a wide frequency range, thus are responsible for <a href="/wiki/Scotopic_vision" title="Scotopic vision">black-and-white vision</a>. Rod cells are not present on the <a href="/wiki/Fovea_centralis" title="Fovea centralis">fovea</a>, the area of the retina responsible for central vision, and are not as responsive as cone cells to spatial and temporal changes in light. There are, however, twenty times more rod cells than cone cells in the retina because the rod cells are present across a wider area. Because of their wider distribution, rods are responsible for <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_vision" title="Peripheral vision">peripheral vision</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>In contrast, cone cells are less sensitive to the overall intensity of light, but come in three varieties that are sensitive to different frequency-ranges and thus are used in the perception of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour" title="Colour">colour</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photopic_vision" title="Photopic vision">photopic vision</a>. Cone cells are highly concentrated in the fovea and have a high visual acuity meaning that they are better at spatial resolution than rod cells. Since cone cells are not as sensitive to dim light as rod cells, most <a href="/wiki/Night_vision" title="Night vision">night vision</a> is limited to rod cells. Likewise, since cone cells are in the fovea, central vision (including the vision needed to do most reading, fine detail work such as sewing, or careful examination of objects) is done by cone cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kandel_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ciliary_muscle" title="Ciliary muscle">Ciliary muscles</a> around the lens allow the eye's focus to be adjusted. This process is known as <a href="/wiki/Accommodation_(eye)" title="Accommodation (eye)">accommodation</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_point" title="Near point">near point</a> and <a href="/wiki/Far_point" title="Far point">far point</a> define the nearest and farthest distances from the eye at which an object can be brought into sharp focus. For a person with normal vision, the far point is located at infinity. The near point's location depends on how much the muscles can increase the curvature of the lens, and how inflexible the lens has become with age. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optometrist" title="Optometrist">Optometrists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophthalmologist" title="Ophthalmologist">ophthalmologists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Optician" title="Optician">opticians</a> usually consider an appropriate near point to be closer than normal reading distance—approximately 25 cm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eyeoptics_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-eyeoptics-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Defects in vision can be explained using optical principles. As people age, the lens becomes less flexible and the near point recedes from the eye, a condition known as <a href="/wiki/Presbyopia" title="Presbyopia">presbyopia</a>. Similarly, people suffering from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperopia" title="Hyperopia">hyperopia</a> cannot decrease the focal length of their lens enough to allow for nearby objects to be imaged on their retina. Conversely, people who cannot increase the focal length of their lens enough to allow for distant objects to be imaged on the retina suffer from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myopia" title="Myopia">myopia</a> and have a far point that is considerably closer than infinity. A condition known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astigmatism_(eye)" title="Astigmatism (eye)">astigmatism</a> results when the cornea is not spherical but instead is more curved in one direction. This causes horizontally extended objects to be focused on different parts of the retina than vertically extended objects, and results in distorted images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eyeoptics_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-eyeoptics-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>All of these conditions can be corrected using <a href="/wiki/Corrective_lens" title="Corrective lens">corrective lenses</a>. For presbyopia and hyperopia, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Converging_lens" title="Converging lens">converging lens</a> provides the extra curvature necessary to bring the near point closer to the eye while for myopia a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diverging_lens" title="Diverging lens">diverging lens</a> provides the curvature necessary to send the far point to infinity. Astigmatism is corrected with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" title="Cylinder (geometry)">cylindrical surface</a> lens that curves more strongly in one direction than in another, compensating for the non-uniformity of the cornea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lensdesign_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-lensdesign-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The optical power of corrective lenses is measured in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diopter" title="Diopter">diopters</a>, a value equal to the <a href="/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse" title="Multiplicative inverse">reciprocal</a> of the focal length measured in metres; with a positive focal length corresponding to a converging lens and a negative focal length corresponding to a diverging lens. For lenses that correct for astigmatism as well, three numbers are given: one for the spherical power, one for the cylindrical power, and one for the angle of orientation of the astigmatism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lensdesign_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-lensdesign-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_effects">Visual effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Visual effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_illusions" title="Optical illusions">Optical illusions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perspective_(graphical)" title="Perspective (graphical)">Perspective (graphical)</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For the visual effects used in film, video, and computer graphics, see <a href="/wiki/Visual_effects" title="Visual effects">visual effects</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ponzo_illusion.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="166" data-file-width="225" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Ponzo_illusion.gif/220px-Ponzo_illusion.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Ponzo_illusion.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ponzo_illusion.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Ponzo Illusion relies on the fact that parallel lines appear to converge as they approach infinity.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Optical illusions (also called visual illusions) are characterized by <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">visually perceived</a> images that differ from objective reality. The information gathered by the eye is processed in the brain to give a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percept" title="Percept">percept</a> that differs from the object being imaged. Optical illusions can be the result of a variety of phenomena including physical effects that create images that are different from the objects that make them, the physiological effects on the eyes and brain of excessive stimulation (e.g. brightness, tilt, colour, movement), and cognitive illusions where the eye and brain make <a href="/wiki/Unconscious_inference" title="Unconscious inference">unconscious inferences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cognitive illusions include some which result from the unconscious misapplication of certain optical principles. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Ames_room" title="Ames room">Ames room</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hering_illusion" title="Hering illusion">Hering</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCller-Lyer_illusion" title="Müller-Lyer illusion">Müller-Lyer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbison%27s_illusion" title="Orbison's illusion">Orbison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ponzo_illusion" title="Ponzo illusion">Ponzo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sander_illusion" title="Sander illusion">Sander</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wundt_illusion" title="Wundt illusion">Wundt illusions</a> all rely on the suggestion of the appearance of distance by using converging and diverging lines, in the same way that parallel light rays (or indeed any set of parallel lines) appear to converge at a <a href="/wiki/Vanishing_point" title="Vanishing point">vanishing point</a> at infinity in two-dimensionally rendered <a href="/wiki/Perspective_(graphical)" title="Perspective (graphical)">images with artistic perspective</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> This suggestion is also responsible for the famous <a href="/wiki/Moon_illusion" title="Moon illusion">moon illusion</a> where the moon, despite having essentially the same angular size, appears much larger near the <a href="/wiki/Horizon" title="Horizon">horizon</a> than it does at <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> This illusion so confounded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_of_Alexandria" title="Ptolemy of Alexandria">Ptolemy</a> that he incorrectly attributed it to atmospheric refraction when he described it in his treatise, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optics_(Ptolemy)" title="Optics (Ptolemy)">Optics</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ptolemy_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ptolemy-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another type of optical illusion exploits broken patterns to trick the mind into perceiving symmetries or asymmetries that are not present. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_wall_illusion" title="Café wall illusion">café wall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ehrenstein_illusion" title="Ehrenstein illusion">Ehrenstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraser_spiral_illusion" title="Fraser spiral illusion">Fraser spiral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poggendorff_illusion" title="Poggendorff illusion">Poggendorff</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%B6llner_illusion" title="Zöllner illusion">Zöllner illusions</a>. Related, but not strictly illusions, are patterns that occur due to the superimposition of periodic structures. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transparency_(optics)" title="Transparency (optics)">transparent</a> tissues with a grid structure produce shapes known as <a href="/wiki/Moir%C3%A9_pattern" title="Moiré pattern">moiré patterns</a>, while the superimposition of periodic transparent patterns comprising parallel opaque lines or curves produces <a href="/wiki/Line_moir%C3%A9" title="Line moiré">line moiré</a> patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Optical_instruments">Optical instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Optical instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Table_of_Opticks,_Cyclopaedia,_Volume_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2899" data-file-width="1789" height="486" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Table_of_Opticks%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_Volume_2.jpg/300px-Table_of_Opticks%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_Volume_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Table_of_Opticks%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_Volume_2.jpg/450px-Table_of_Opticks%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_Volume_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Table_of_Opticks%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_Volume_2.jpg/600px-Table_of_Opticks%2C_Cyclopaedia%2C_Volume_2.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Table_of_Opticks,_Cyclopaedia,_Volume_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustrations of various optical instruments from the 1728 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclopaedia,_or_an_Universal_Dictionary_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Cyclopaedia, or an Universal Dictionary of Arts and Sciences">Cyclopaedia</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_instruments" title="Optical instruments">Optical instruments</a></div>
<p>Single lenses have a variety of applications including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photographic_lens" title="Photographic lens">photographic lenses</a>, corrective lenses, and <a href="/wiki/Magnifying_glass" title="Magnifying glass">magnifying glasses</a> while single mirrors are used in <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic reflectors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rear-view_mirror" title="Rear-view mirror">rear-view mirrors</a>. Combining a number of mirrors, prisms, and lenses produces compound optical instruments which have practical uses. For example, a <a href="/wiki/Periscope" title="Periscope">periscope</a> is simply two plane mirrors aligned to allow for viewing around obstructions. The most famous compound optical instruments in science are the <a href="/wiki/Optical_microscope" title="Optical microscope">microscope</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">telescope</a> which were both invented by the Dutch in the late 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instrument_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-instrument-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Microscopes were first developed with just two lenses: an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Objective_lens" title="Objective lens">objective lens</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Eyepiece" title="Eyepiece">eyepiece</a>. The objective lens is essentially a magnifying glass and was designed with a very small <a href="/wiki/Focal_length" title="Focal length">focal length</a> while the eyepiece generally has a longer focal length. This has the effect of producing magnified images of close objects. Generally, an additional source of illumination is used since magnified images are dimmer due to the <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of energy</a> and the spreading of light rays over a larger surface area. Modern microscopes, known as <i>compound microscopes</i> have many lenses in them (typically four) to optimize the functionality and enhance image stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instrument_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-instrument-86">[86]</a></sup> A slightly different variety of microscope, the <a href="/wiki/Comparison_microscope" title="Comparison microscope">comparison microscope</a>, looks at side-by-side images to produce a <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereoscopic</a> <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular</a> view that appears three dimensional when used by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first telescopes, called <i><a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">refracting telescopes</a></i> were also developed with a single objective and eyepiece lens. In contrast to the microscope, the objective lens of the telescope was designed with a large focal length to avoid optical aberrations. The objective focuses an image of a distant object at its focal point which is adjusted to be at the focal point of an eyepiece of a much smaller focal length. The main goal of a telescope is not necessarily magnification, but rather collection of light which is determined by the physical size of the objective lens. Thus, telescopes are normally indicated by the diameters of their objectives rather than by the magnification which can be changed by switching eyepieces. Because the magnification of a telescope is equal to the focal length of the objective divided by the focal length of the eyepiece, smaller focal-length eyepieces cause greater magnification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instrument_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-instrument-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since crafting large lenses is much more difficult than crafting large mirrors, most modern telescopes are <i><a href="/wiki/Reflecting_telescope" title="Reflecting telescope">reflecting telescopes</a></i>, that is, telescopes that use a primary mirror rather than an objective lens. The same general optical considerations apply to reflecting telescopes that applied to refracting telescopes, namely, the larger the primary mirror, the more light collected, and the magnification is still equal to the focal length of the primary mirror divided by the focal length of the eyepiece. Professional telescopes generally do not have eyepieces and instead place an instrument (often a charge-coupled device) at the focal point instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instrument_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-instrument-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photography">Photography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Photography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_of_photography" title="Science of photography">Science of photography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jonquil_flowers_at_f32.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1054" data-file-width="1600" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Jonquil_flowers_at_f32.jpg/300px-Jonquil_flowers_at_f32.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Jonquil_flowers_at_f32.jpg/450px-Jonquil_flowers_at_f32.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Jonquil_flowers_at_f32.jpg/600px-Jonquil_flowers_at_f32.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jonquil_flowers_at_f5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1054" data-file-width="1600" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Jonquil_flowers_at_f5.jpg/300px-Jonquil_flowers_at_f5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Jonquil_flowers_at_f5.jpg/450px-Jonquil_flowers_at_f5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Jonquil_flowers_at_f5.jpg/600px-Jonquil_flowers_at_f5.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jonquil_flowers_at_f5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photograph taken with aperture <span style="font-style:italic;font-family:Trebuchet MS,Candara,Georgia,Calibri,Corbel,serif">f</span>/5</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The optics of <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a> involves both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photographic_lens" title="Photographic lens">lenses</a> and the medium in which the electromagnetic radiation is recorded, whether it be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photographic_plates" title="Photographic plates">plate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photographic_film" title="Photographic film">film</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">charge-coupled device</a>. Photographers must consider the <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(photography)" title="Reciprocity (photography)">reciprocity</a> of the camera and the shot which is summarized by the relation</p>
<dl>
<dd>Exposure ∝ ApertureArea × ExposureTime × SceneLuminance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>In other words, the smaller the aperture (giving greater depth of focus), the less light coming in, so the length of time has to be increased (leading to possible blurriness if motion occurs). An example of the use of the law of reciprocity is the <a href="/wiki/Sunny_16_rule" title="Sunny 16 rule">Sunny 16 rule</a> which gives a rough estimate for the settings needed to estimate the proper <a href="/wiki/Exposure_(photography)" title="Exposure (photography)">exposure</a> in daylight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>A camera's aperture is measured by a unitless number called the <a href="/wiki/F-number" title="F-number">f-number</a> or f-stop, <span style="font-style:italic;font-family:Trebuchet MS,Candara,Georgia,Calibri,Corbel,serif">f</span>/#, often notated as <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>N</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle N}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="N" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5e3890c981ae85503089652feb48b191b57aae3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.074ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, and given by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
