</math></span><img alt="f/\# = N = \frac fD \ " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8c57b73a41758446ebf19496902fad6c375a597b" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:16.052ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/132e57acb643253e7810ee9702d9581f159a1c61" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.289ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Focal_length" title="Focal length">focal length</a>, and <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>D</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle D}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="D" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f34a0c600395e5d4345287e21fb26efd386990e6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.935ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the diameter of the entrance pupil. By convention, "<span style="font-style:italic;font-family:Trebuchet MS,Candara,Georgia,Calibri,Corbel,serif">f</span>/#" is treated as a single symbol, and specific values of <span style="font-style:italic;font-family:Trebuchet MS,Candara,Georgia,Calibri,Corbel,serif">f</span>/# are written by replacing the <a href="/wiki/Number_sign" title="Number sign">number sign</a> with the value. The two ways to increase the f-stop are to either decrease the diameter of the entrance pupil or change to a longer focal length (in the case of a <a href="/wiki/Zoom_lens" title="Zoom lens">zoom lens</a>, this can be done by simply adjusting the lens). Higher f-numbers also have a larger <a href="/wiki/Depth_of_field" title="Depth of field">depth of field</a> due to the lens approaching the limit of a <a href="/wiki/Pinhole_camera" title="Pinhole camera">pinhole camera</a> which is able to focus all images perfectly, regardless of distance, but requires very long exposure times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The field of view that the lens will provide changes with the focal length of the lens. There are three basic classifications based on the relationship to the diagonal size of the film or sensor size of the camera to the focal length of the lens:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce_Warren.2C_Photography.2C_page_71_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce_Warren.2C_Photography.2C_page_71-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Normal_lens" title="Normal lens">Normal lens</a>: angle of view of about 50° (called <i>normal</i> because this angle considered roughly equivalent to human vision<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce_Warren.2C_Photography.2C_page_71_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce_Warren.2C_Photography.2C_page_71-91">[91]</a></sup>) and a focal length approximately equal to the diagonal of the film or sensor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wide-angle_lens" title="Wide-angle lens">Wide-angle lens</a>: angle of view wider than 60° and focal length shorter than a normal lens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_focus_lens" title="Long focus lens">Long focus lens</a>: angle of view narrower than a normal lens. This is any lens with a focal length longer than the diagonal measure of the film or sensor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The most common type of long focus lens is the <a href="/wiki/Telephoto_lens" title="Telephoto lens">telephoto lens</a>, a design that uses a special <i>telephoto group</i> to be physically shorter than its focal length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Zoom_lens" title="Zoom lens">zoom lenses</a> may have some or all of these attributes.</p>
<p>The absolute value for the exposure time required depends on how <a href="/wiki/Sensitometry" title="Sensitometry">sensitive</a> to light the medium being used is (measured by the <a href="/wiki/Film_speed" title="Film speed">film speed</a>, or, for digital media, by the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_efficiency" title="Quantum efficiency">quantum efficiency</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Early photography used media that had very low light sensitivity, and so exposure times had to be long even for very bright shots. As technology has improved, so has the sensitivity through film cameras and digital cameras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other results from physical and geometrical optics apply to camera optics. For example, the maximum resolution capability of a particular camera set-up is determined by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diffraction_limit" title="Diffraction limit">diffraction limit</a> associated with the pupil size and given, roughly, by the Rayleigh criterion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_optics">Atmospheric optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Optics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Atmospheric optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_optics" title="Atmospheric optics">Atmospheric optics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Firesunset2edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Firesunset2edit.jpg/300px-Firesunset2edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Firesunset2edit.jpg/450px-Firesunset2edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Firesunset2edit.jpg/600px-Firesunset2edit.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Firesunset2edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A colourful sky is often due to scattering of light off particulates and pollution, as in this photograph of a sunset during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/October_2007_California_wildfires" title="October 2007 California wildfires">October 2007 California wildfires</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The unique optical properties of the atmosphere cause a wide range of spectacular optical phenomena. The blue colour of the sky is a direct result of <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a> which redirects higher frequency (blue) sunlight back into the field of view of the observer. Because blue light is scattered more easily than red light, the sun takes on a reddish hue when it is observed through a thick atmosphere, as during a <a href="/wiki/Sunrise" title="Sunrise">sunrise</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sunset" title="Sunset">sunset</a>. Additional particulate matter in the sky can scatter different colours at different angles creating colourful glowing skies at dusk and dawn. Scattering off of ice crystals and other particles in the atmosphere are responsible for <a href="/wiki/Halo_(optical_phenomenon)" title="Halo (optical phenomenon)">halos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afterglow" title="Afterglow">afterglows</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corona_(meteorology)" title="Corona (meteorology)">coronas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular_rays" title="Crepuscular rays">rays of sunlight</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sun_dog" title="Sun dog">sun dogs</a>. The variation in these kinds of phenomena is due to different particle sizes and geometries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mirage" title="Mirage">Mirages</a> are optical phenomena in which light rays are bent due to thermal variations in the refraction index of air, producing displaced or heavily distorted images of distant objects. Other dramatic optical phenomena associated with this include the <a href="/wiki/Novaya_Zemlya_effect" title="Novaya Zemlya effect">Novaya Zemlya effect</a> where the sun appears to rise earlier than predicted with a distorted shape. A spectacular form of refraction occurs with a <a href="/wiki/Inversion_(meteorology)" title="Inversion (meteorology)">temperature inversion</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Fata_Morgana_(mirage)" title="Fata Morgana (mirage)">Fata Morgana</a> where objects on the horizon or even beyond the horizon, such as islands, cliffs, ships or icebergs, appear elongated and elevated, like "fairy tale castles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">Rainbows</a> are the result of a combination of internal reflection and dispersive refraction of light in raindrops. A single reflection off the backs of an array of raindrops produces a rainbow with an angular size on the sky that ranges from 40° to 42° with red on the outside. Double rainbows are produced by two internal reflections with angular size of 50.5° to 54° with violet on the outside. Because rainbows are seen with the sun 180° away from the centre of the rainbow, rainbows are more prominent the closer the sun is to the horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-light_66-5"><a href="#cite_note-light-66">[66]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-j1-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-j1_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-j1_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Rashed, Roshdi (1990). "A pioneer in anaclastics: Ibn Sahl on burning mirrors and lenses". <i>Isis</i>. <b>81</b> (3): 464–491. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1086%2F355456" rel="nofollow">10.1086/355456</a>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/233423" rel="nofollow">233423</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOptics&amp;rft.atitle=A+pioneer+in+anaclastics%3A+Ibn+Sahl+on+burning+mirrors+and+lenses&amp;rft.aufirst=Roshdi&amp;rft.aulast=Rashed&amp;rft.date=1990&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F233423&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1086%2F355456&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Isis&amp;rft.pages=464-491&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=81"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
